0439023513|3.0|**Possible Spoilers Ahead**<br /><br />One of my close friends often teases me for not liking happy endings in novels. That's not true, I argue back--I don't dislike happy endings, but they have to feel earned. And what reader doesn't appreciate the sharp pang of the bittersweet?<br /><br />So, on to the review: As I read the final chapter of _Mockingjay_, and then the (very rushed) epilogue, I felt conflicted. Confused. Dissatisfied. And not necessarily in a &#34;good&#34; way. As if I'd eaten one of Peeta's cakes after realizing that someone had poured a bag of salt into the batter.<br /><br />I did not expect a fairytale wrap-up. I did not expect major characters to come out of the rebellion unscathed, or even alive. What I did expect, however, was a satisfying conclusion--at least in the literary sense. In Book 1, Collins created a dystopian world with so much potential. The writing was edgy, direct, and fast-paced. The plot an ironic commentary on--and criticism of--contemporary life and media culture. Book 3, however, focuses on the tragic spectacle of war while leaving character development by the wayside.<br /><br />In _The Hunger Games_, Katniss was practical, reckless, intelligent, stubborn, and ironic. Sensitive, yet single-minded to a fault. Inexperienced in matters of the heart, but trying to understand what it means to have an intimate relationship with another human being, especially when immersed in oppression and artifice. In short, she was someone the reader could relate to. Were we not drawn to her voice, her perspective, her personal trials? Now, I'm not saying that seventeen-year-old _Mockingjay_ Katniss should be exempt from the psychological trauma of war. Anyone would cave under the pressure of becoming a symbol for twelve rebelling districts--i.e., civilization. But when, for three-fourths of Book 3, Katniss is in an emaciated, depressed, drug-induced, and/or mentally unstable state...Needless to say, this depiction leaves much to be desired. I wanted to see Katniss cut her own puppet strings and grow into the role of a leader. Above all else, Katniss is a fighter. A survivor. And much more eloquent than she gives herself credit for. No more morphling. No more hiding in drain pipes or supply closets. Maybe I am really a sucker for &#34;happy endings,&#34; but I didn't want Katniss to just survive. I wanted her to *live.* Perhaps, in the end, circumstances would never allow it. Perhaps the fire was simply too big for her to control.<br /><br />Another thing. One of the best aspects of Hunger Games is that the romantic relationships are not at the forefront of the story. In fact, the &#34;love triangle&#34; is, in my opinion, the backdrop to a much bigger theme: the meaning of humanity in a post-apocalyptic landscape. I wasn't particularly interested in the Gale vs. Peeta debate, although I liked both characters and wanted to see what roles they would play. I was mainly interested in how Katniss would work out her complex feelings and deal with her own emotions. There was a golden opportunity to develop the personalities of these characters and explore their dynamic in more depth, yet the reality fell short of the mark (my thoughts on Peeta post-capture, and Gale post-rebellion, have already been voiced by other reviewers). Also, we are introduced to new characters who do not necessarily contribute to the overall storyline, while old characters either reappear for the sake of it or are not given their dues. Furthermore, I have many problems with District 13--especially with President Coin and her role in the rebellion. Additionally, the death of a certain character near the end of _Mockingjay_ makes Katniss' actions in the first book seem very pointless. Did this person really need to die? And in such a short-changed way? Also, where is the battle of wits and final confrontation with Snow? The Grand Finale? Despite the epilogue, it does not seem like much has changed once the rebellion ends. For some reason, I can't shake the feeling that Panem's history might repeat itself and that Katniss' &#34;victory&#34; will offer only a brief respite from everyone's self-destructive tendencies.<br /><br />One of the major flaws of this series, I believe, is the fact that it is a trilogy. If _Catching Fire_ and _Mockingjay_ had somehow been condensed into one book (and this means no Quarter Quell, no Coin, and no underground District 13)--then evidently we would have a very different story. Book 3 is indeed the culmination of Panem's political brutality and social unrest, but without the tight plotting and emotional payoff that a story of this magnitude requires. Don't get me wrong. I enjoyed reading all three books, and the ideas simultaneously entertained me, intrigued me, and disturbed me. But is _Mockingjay_, the great deathsong of the Capitol, a five-star masterpiece? No. A one-star total flop? Again--no. But oh, it could have been so much more...<br /><br />True or not true? Your move, reader. After all, there are much worse games to play.y might repeat itself and that Katniss' &#34;victory&#34; will offer only a brief respite from everyone's self-destructive tendencies. B005CYLSW8|5.0|I'll amend this review later with more blow-by-blow detail.  Suffice it to say now, though, that this new Beethoven cycle from Chailly and the GOL will firmly be in the top tier for many serious listeners and collectors, as well as the novice who may have a few of the symphonies by various performers, but not yet a complete set/cycle.  Amongst many followers of musicological fashion, the de rigeur of the new Del Mar/Baerenreiter urtext has dominated discussion of current Beethoven performance practice, so it's worth noting that Chailly has used the \\"old\\" Peters Edition, but with an exactitude that has eluded--usually by design--several generations of conductors.  Performing traditions have added layer upon layer of editorial gloss--\\"Karajan always drew out this figure,\\" \\"Furtwangler started this accelerando two beats before the mark,\\" that sort of thing--and indeed, Chailly's game was to seek out and examine the mark-ups by several of his musical mentors, weigh the evidence....and then land squarely on the side of Beethoven's \\"original\\" text (specifically the Peters edit thereof).    Brave words from a reviewer, and an even braver stand for a conductor--a rather facile postion that seldom survives scrutiny.  Nevertheless, this is precisely what Chailly has done over the term of this multi-year studio cycle (no live relay patches here....hooray!).  Many was the time that I went running to my miniature scores to see if what I'd heard was there--it's that startling at times--but rarely did Chailly come up short vs. the dynamic markings or the repeats (pretty much ALL of them).  More importantly, though, was his attention to Beethoven's \\"controversial\\" crochet indications:  startling tempos to match the eyebrow-raising dynamics.  This is where we get deep into the weeds of original-performance controversy, as well as the old saws about how for whatever reason, \\"Beethoven didn't really mean it,\\" or words to that effect.  Scholars, performers, and listeners have been apologising for Beethoven's score instructions for 200 years, and so once again, here we go with another conductor who's promising to scrub the score clean from all the accumulated Victorian hagiographic varnish (the \\"Beethoven for the Oldsmobile\\" approach that drapes the composer in black and hangs him over the parlour mantle) and conductorial second-guessing and point-making.  When done well, the old approach yielded Furtwangler, Klemperer, Bernstein, Karajan and the like--much received tradition and hitting of all the right tick-marks; done badly, and then it's pretty much any conductor of middling rank who seems mired in the mud.  The Period Performance crowd have their own idols, with Hogwood, Norrington, Harnoncourt and Gardiner leading the publicity charge over the last 30 years.  In the middle ground, Vanska & the Minnesota band:  lean textures, brighter winds and brass, minimal vibrato, the new Del Mar edition score--a justly well-received blend of new scholarship and traditional presentation without the fat.  So given all this, where do Chailly and the Gewandhaus Orchestra of Leipzig come in?  Like Vanska, tempos are brisk, rhythms are crisp, dynamics are precisely observed.  But in Chailly's hands, we really do hear the Italianate brio of Toscanini--perhaps filtered by the watchful eye of a Gardiner.  Here indeed is where Chailly knits things together.  The inner rhythms that Beethoven stretched over multiple bars come alive in the quick tempos--faithful to Beethoven's challenging crochet markings--and sharply-etched dynamics.  The long lines sing out with an almost Rossinian (I can see the brows tightening) bel canto, swept forward with a pinache that stands in sharp contrast to lesser didacticians like Norrington.  Gone are the pregnant pauses inserted for melodramatic emphasis over the years, the swooning swells and close-ups that were more appropriate to the Hollywood soundstages.  With Chailly, you can really hear the debt to Haydn being put paid by theclose of Symphony #1; the truly revolutionary structure of \\"Eroica\\" with its still-arresting crags and cul-de-sacs of dissonance--relieved at the last second by triumphant resolution; and thunder-claps of tympany in the scherzo of the 9th, not to mention the truly rapturous singing in the choral finale.  And, the genius of it all is that most of the heavy lifting is accomplished by sticking closely to Beethoven's markings.    No sonic Listerine to weather. Chailly's steel strings are well-informed by period practice (lean textures, minimal vibrato, and 1st/2nd L/R division), the winds are pungent, and the brass all but bark on occasion (when called for) in the best valveless style--though all such touches are tempered by an almost vocal quality to the long line.  Yet, even more so than with Gardiner, there is a palpable excitement and \\"newness\\" to the proceedings.  Vanska and Gardiner are close cousins here, though I think the palm goes to Chailly.  The GOL of Kurt Masur in his hey-day was never this nimble, never this alive.  Looking at my listening notes, I keep running across references to articulation in the low strings I'd never heard before, or wind figurations in counterpoint with the long line of the aforesaid strings....that sort of thing.  The Eroica's funeral march is very much a military one, no longer suggestive of Prince Albert draped in black on his way to George's Chapel (I ran back to my old Karajan 1962 set--still a good one--and was promptly appalled all over again at the agogic slowness, fashionable for generations of listeners and concertgoers).  The impression here, and indeed throughout the whole cycle of symphonies and a generous ration of overtures, is of a truly fine orchestra asked to do the extraordinary, to re-mold its signature burnished tone into a quicksilver tool at Chailly's disposal, equally committed to realising a performance vision.  It's what I'd hoped to hear with Rattle's recent cycle with the VPO, but you can hear the orchestra fighting him from time to time.  None of that here.  Lest I wax on further with the performances, on to the sound.  We've a beautifully wide and deep soundstage, as well as a strong but well-managed hall ambience--a GOL signature left intact.  It reminded me of John Culshaw working in Kingsway Hall, but with a more elegant/plush/upholstered sound, and more detailed.  I don't have a desk-count for violins, but it feels like about half-strength, making the winds and brass more \\"present\\" without being spotlit.  This also yields the dividend noted above, with the astonishing business of the cellos and basses allowed to \\"tell\\" without coming through as a low-pitched smear.  You really do marvel at what serves as Beethoven's \\"motor,\\" the precise bowing generating a virtual heartbeat of articulated motion.  Less a product of discreet multi-miking than well-captured orchestral technique, this simple example of sonic clarity balanced from the podium bolsters Chailly's vision.  Price-wise the set is still a bargain, even though now more than the $35.80 I paid through Amazon's pre-order (look at their secondary sellers for a better break).  Yet, compared to other full sets--not to mention the very nice hardbound booklet-with-sleeves album presentation style--it remains a very good deal.  Closest comparison will be Vanska, as detailed above, though the monetary outlay for the latter is significantly more.  I'm still keeping my old Karajan '62 set, along with Gardiner and Harnoncourt, my Vanska singles & various dribs and drabs from other worthies. The Symphonies have and will always summon great performances.  But--and here's another listening notes observation--it's rare that I've smiled ear to ear as a Beethoven cycle progressed in front of me on first hearing.  And the second, and the third.  All the usual Amazon suspects will no doubt weigh in with correctives and citations ad nauseum, and that's all to the good.  It means we're paying attention, and it is after all the\\"core\\" of the core repetoire.  And, to be fair, I've been underwhelmed by Chailly in the past (his Mahler is VERY hit-and-miss).  Nevertheless, THIS is truly a fine effort.  The doubters may be in for a rather pleasant surprise.

The guide gives some pretty generic tips for creating your character; good for a first-time TES player, but not so much for someone who has played out the series. After many pages of very basic base information on the different races, the guide pretty much sums up the best advice possible: Pick a race that has strengths that suit your style of play. A bit more detail in what race features play into the different play types would have been good, but to be fair I think they did a pretty good job when you consider all the different styles of play that exist to group play styles into the main categories they did. I was looking for a lot more based on the hype about all the changes and the level of experience I have had with the other TES games. In relation to my expectations of both, I was disappointed, but only because I now realize that the changes were not as monumental as they were made to seem. So on this note; I give the guide a \\"C-\\". Maybe they should re-write the description of the guide to mention it provides \\"beginner\\" character creation tips instead of saying \\"must-have\\" tips.  My second beef is with the font size. It is pretty small. Add to that the fact that most who play the game will play ina darkened room to eliminate screen glare and make it easier for your eyes to adjust to some of the darker scenes in the game and you have a guide that is not as handy for in-game play as it could be. Artistically, the guide looks pretty good in a well-lit room when you are thumbing through it. The pictures jump out at you and it only takes a second to figure out the formatting of the text layout. In the dark, though, the guide loses all the appeal it had in that brightly-lit room! I understand the book is massive and they had to use a smaller font to fit all the info into as few pages as possible; but really? The tables and reference portions are the features I tried to use most and the fact that the index and tables uses an even smaller font than the walkthroughs' font is very disheartening. First I have to break out the mini LED flashlight and then squint to read the table! Maybe I should have bought one of those lighted reading magnifiers... My grade on this review portion is a \\"D\\".  My third area of discontent isn't really that bad, but I only say that because I haven't used the guide for walkthroughs at all yet (I read through the character creation portion and use the reference tables once in a while). I'm talking about grammatical and spelling errors. While I suspect grammar and spelling aren't exactly the defining properties of a video game-guide, I still have to mention that I have seen way to many in the very small portion of the guide I have used. If the entire guide is as riddled with them as the portions I have seen, then it is definitely a flaw with the guide. I do realize, though, that editing something like this would be very difficult. Spelling errors should be easily spotted, but grammatical errors would be difficult unless you had actually played the game and had experienced the situation being described in the guide. I guess some spelling errors could be attributed to this reasoning as well. It is hard for an author to edit his or her own work because he/she always reads what he/she had intended to write instead of what he/she actually wrote. The grade for this is \\"B-\\" because I am presuming the problem is widespread but don't know for sure.  My last complaint is the index. I think the publisher could have done much better with it. To have a (roughly) 700 page guide with an index consisting of only 4 or 5 pages is tragic. A good index should provide multiple entries for each indexed point when there are multiple ways to look up the info. The publisher already decreased the font-size, then listed entries in a limited way. This detracts substantially from the use of the guide if you were to actually use it as a walkthrough. My teenage son, who has no issue with a walkthrough since his goal is to \\"beat\\" the game as quickly as possible, has mentioned the lacking of the index more than a few times. (Old guy's side note: How do you consider it beating the game if someone else had to tell you how to succeed?) Final grade on the index: \\"C\\".  Now, I think the tables I use for reference are very good; \\"A-\\". There is no way to get all the info possible into a mass-market publication, so I think the publisher and game guide author did a very good job in this area. Also, as I alluded to earlier, the look of the guide is pretty good in an artistic sense; \\"A-\\". Pictures of NPC faces all over seems a bit of overkill to me, but hey, I remember playing Zork and actually reading my video game!  My overall review is 3 stars because I can't knock the guide that much when it is the first guide I have ever bought and have nothing to compare it to. Improvement opportunities exist, but it looks like it would do the job that most would expect it to do. Happy gamming everyone; and my final bit of advice is try finishing the game on expert and not using the internet, a game-guide, or a bunch of input from your friends to finish the game. The satisfaction is so much more gratifying! And if nothing else, think of the bragging rights you would have...

B005VR9328|4.0|The Roots have long been building up to the glowing crossover reaction they've received in favor of their tenth proper studio album, a just-released concept record called undun. In black and white, it seems so obvious now, looking back. Simply put, The Roots have always wanted to be more than a hip-hop group. They've always wanted to make more than average albums (remember Phrenology?) and, well, they've always wanted to be big beyond what the confines of The Source readership could offer. With their unlikely gig as Jimmy Fallon's house band now firmly celebrated by the average alternative music writer and the late night viewer alike, they've now become the household name that making boom bap hip-hop records never allowed them. They've become, one could argue, the most constant and positive symbol for hip-hop music in popular world culture. And the timing is perfect. After years of making bloated, spotty record that seriously lacked diversity, they've now made two classic records in a row (the other being last year's excellent How I Got Over), thanks in part, we can only assume, to the diversity their Fallon gig has demanded of them.  For example: at the end of undun there's a four-part suite that opens with a Sufjan Stevens piano loop. Big deal, right? Well, it is. It's further proof that The Roots have opened up to music of all sorts, these last two records even suggesting that they're fans of the current indie rock scene (How I Got Over feature prominent Joanna Newsom and Monsters of Folk samples). But, I wonder, are The Roots genuine? I'm suspicious, mostly of drummer ?uestlove, a guy who has very obviously always been interested in celebrity. For example: is there a better way to get the attention of the reviewers with the loudest voices than to sample their favorite artists on their records? (Will Bradford Cox be on the next Roots record? The National?) But, all the fame-as-business-strategy stuff aside, undun is one of the best hip-hop records of the year andmost definitely one of the best Roots albums yet.  More mellow and musical than their early work, the band continues to tighten things up with their tenth record, offering up a modest nine full songs, an intro, an interlude and the above-mentioned four-part suite. All together, the record passes quickly and smoothly, still indebted to jazz, soul and classic era boom bap music more than any other genre. One again, the band incorporates quite a few R&B hooks, none of which work nearly as well as Erykah Badu's now-classic contribution to \\"You Got Me.\\" One longstanding problem the band recently fixed relates to their powerful frontman, Black Thought. One of the great emcees of all-time, BT has never been a diverse emcee, nor one who could carry the vocal weight of a full album. And so now we have many, many vocal guests. Mostly, we get the great Dice Raw, Phonte and Greg Porn. But others, too, including Bilal, Big K.R.I.T. and someone named Truck North. Porn and Raw work the best, their styles fitting in nicely with the crew's vibe and Black Thought's vocal leadership.  By the time the album ends we've worked our way through the loosely illustrated life story of a fictional character named Redford Stephens. But, really, what we get is something of a New York City chronicle that works as a more literate - if less passionate - continuation of the New York City Nas painted on Illmatic. It's a gritty, musical and tight record full of great compositions, classic beats and great verses. But, all that said, The Roots latest album, no matter what you heard from all your favorite indie rock writers, is not a perfect album.  My biggest issue with undun is the same thing I felt kept How I Got Over from being one of the great hip-hop records of all time. And that problem is one that could so easily be fixed - these Roots guys just flat out have bad taste in hooks. They pick cheesy R&B singers and they write cheesy hooks. Get Q-Tip to do your hooks. Get D'Angelo. Get Charles Bradley or even Robert Bradley. Sample old soul records. Hearing these timeless, organic compositions - and Black Thought's gritty verses - juxtaposed against soft, overly-poppy and characterless R&B vocals is a major turn off. Well, for me that is. But yes, I do still like undun despite this one major fault. It's a great hip-hop record that stands as the band's tightest and most realized to date. Probably not better than How I Got Over, but easily their second best top-to-bottom listen.  Check out more of my music- and film-related writing at ZeCatalist.com.

Late one night Walter Hartright, a young and talented artist, decides to take a leisurely walk, when he stumbles upon a wild beast of unparalleled strength and viciousness, with bloodstained fangs that would make a Balrog seem...  sorry, actually he only happens across a woman dressed entirely in white.  Following this chance encounter he learns that the woman was actually an escaped mental patient, which causes temporary distress, although Walter is confident that the event will have no bearing on his future.  Oh, the naivety so often displayed by main characters.  Almost at once Walter finds himself caught in a web of deceit and lies that span several generations and ends in blackmail and more lies, not to mention a ghastly beast with yellow veined eyes and a temperament akin to... sorry.    Okay, so there aren't any beasts in The Woman in White, although the villains are quite beastly.  There is however a compelling mystery with rich characters and plenty of knots to untangle by the end.  It was written with a disciplined pace that helped us really understand all the family history and fully appreciate the motives of the characters.  Some might consider the narrative to drag on, but we appreciated the fact that nothing was rushed.  And while we were able to work out some of the confusion and suspense before the big reveals, many tidbits kept us guessing until the very end.  Another element that made The Woman in White unique was the changing narratives.  The concept is hardly unique, but the premise was that whoever was closest to the main events would be the individual to move the story forward.  For that reason it felt unique.  The changing perspective also added some nice variety and we only wish Mr. Fairlie was able to get some additional face time.   We also came across a pairof characters that made our blood boil, which is almost always a good thing.  Due to completely fictitious contractual obligations we won't mention any names; however we will say that their villainy is comparable to Uriah Heap and Heathcliff.  Overall, The Woman in White kept us engaged until the final page and gave us a new appreciation for the classic mystery novel.

I had one of the cheap FM transmitters previously and the audio quality and poor FM signal generator made it almost unusable. I'll keep this SoundFly AUX and now I have a way to listen to the hundreds of public domain books available for free on the web while I am commuting.  PROS - The signal (programmable to any even or odd FM frequency, e.g., 92.3, 92.4, 92.5, 92.6, etc.) is solid, loud and clear. However, it is FM radio quality audio. Don't expect it to sound as good as a CD or DVD audio though, as no FM radio audio quality can ever match CD or DVD audio. No surprise there, huh? I like that it reads both USB jump drive and SD cards. It displays both folders and files on the menu but seeing a complete Windows type file screen is too much to expect of any small screen unit such as this or an MP player - only so much info will fit in the six or seven lines of text on the display. The blue, lighted display is clear from angles but like so many displays, is hard to make out when I wear my polarized sun glasses.  CONS - Remote is nice but you have to hold it right in front of the main unit. I did manage to get some preferred frequencies set up but it was in no way intuitive. Also, the unit tilts up/down but not left to right. Still, no deal breakers there! Finally, I cannot say that this unit is completely intuitive so you may expect to refer to the manual now and again. The manual is helpful butneither the best I have ever read nor the worst. So, if I understand the instructions correctly the procedure is to set the bookmark, shut off the Soundfly unit and then you can shut down your car - big pain.  BREAKING THE RULES - So I just set my bookmark, shut off the car, and that's it. That procedure has worked OK for me so far! I am also leaving it in the car (parked outside) and it is surviving 20 degree nights just fine too. We'll see what happens when it gets down to zero overnight - stay tuned!  THE LAST WORD - Call me Mr. Obvious but it should be stated that the quality of the audio you hear is important to people and truly objective reviews of audio quality are rare. I can tell you with certainty that what you hear is dependent on three things - the quality of this little unit, your particular FM radio and the quality of the MP files you play through it. If your MP files are noisy, recorded badly or otherwise sub par - no player in the world will make them sound better.   Here's two fictitious reviews to make my point.  1.) \\"The high quality, professionally produced MPeg music file I bought from a web music service played great and sounded wonderful through this unit broadcasting to my car's Bose FM radio.\\" (Useful review)  2.) \\"This MP player/transmitter sounds cruddy and smells funny - don't buy it!\\" (Useless review)  I want to thank others who take the time to review products. I rely heavily on the COLLECTIVE wisdom of the reviews when making my purchases. I hope my reviews are always useful and truthful to you.

This sounds some thing near to concentration in meditation.  When the meditator gets into a deep state of concentration , he is away from every thing else around him.  In the light of what I had experienced in a short space of time  where there was no declaration of a clinical death or a cardiac arrest, but  an experience of a drowning  in a river, there are similarities with the experience of Dr. Richie. Therefore if NDE  recounted by the \\"patient\\" could be  accepted, it has to be the \\"consciousness\\" that had experienced and not the brain, but a separate  entity say a sort of a field of energy which remembers  things the way \\"the patient\\" lived his life until the moment of his cardiac arrest. Therefore once dead the body which is composed of  the, element of water, element of energy, element of wind and  the  element of hard matter remains, which eventually decays and gets absorbed into  their different  elements. B005CYLSW8|3.0|When you've been around music as long a I have - longer than Chailly - you become immured to the hype that surrounds new recordings that arrive amid fanfares. For any \\"latest set\\" over the last 60 years there has been praise as \\"maybe the greatest\\"; yet half of them didn't even survive a normal season of applause. One has to sit down calmly, with the experience of hundreds of hours spent in the company of this composer and his interpreters, to judge a new recording. Having recently been to Leipzig and witnessed the slimming cure this orchestra is presently undergoing (\\"getting rid of the plush\\" was a slogan much bandied about), I heard familiar sounds coming at me from the loudspeakers. Most interesting about this new release is the fact, however, that Masur preceded Chailly with a complete symphonic recording devoted to Beethoven. There was plenty of plush in their playing then, more measured tempi, no hard pushing, but an eminently civilised approach that even then was not to everyone's tastes. But the set has lasted - for the simple reason that it enshrined lasting values. It was not \\"the\\" Beethoven set, but one of the elect at any rate. So how does this new set fare by comparison?  The sound is much better, of course. However, it is a little on the hard side, and not as spacious as before. This can become unpleasant on extended listening. It is not what you would hear people say who just listen to the highlights.  But more worrisome than this are the tempi with which Chailly attacks his work. From the moment that Symphony 1 begins with a brisk Allegretto rather than Adagio, as required by the composer, you know you're going to be in for a spin on the Autobahn! At that same moment you also note that Chailly has a penchant for really punching one-note chords - they are like hammer blows throughout. Finally the main tempo is practically a presto - in some places when a presto is actually prescribed (e.g. Finale of Symphony 7) he can't go any faster.  Altogether histempi are too fast in most movements, including the slow ones - his Eroica Funeral March takes 12' (Barenboim: 18'!). It does not sound grave; in fact it is one of the low points of the set, a very perfunctory rendition, as if he just refused to get emotionally involved in the music. I might as well stay with slow movements; they are the heart of Beethoven and several of them the equivalent of prayers. The only movements where his speed actually works is in the Pastoral and the 7th symphony. The rest of them are banal to the point of sounding like Muzak. There is no sense of exploring Beethoven's spiritually. It seems that having cast the mould, Chailly decided to be consistent and drive down the Autobahn even when he should sit still and meditate. - Taken as a whole, the slow movements are a black mark against this set.  About the head movements: The Eroica is much too fast and consequently sounds like a lightweight. This is not insightful, but erratic. When you come to the 5th ymphony, which is in fact the best of this whole set, this works. But you can't take the 5th as a model for a performance of the 3rd. That is pandering to stereotypes. The 7th, 8th and 9th symphonies are improvements on this routine and some effort made to discriminate between their respective moods. But Chailly is not consistent with this approach: his Pastoral is so breathless it makes you wonder what the word \\"pastoral\\" means to him; and his 4th symphony is neither smiling nor particularly masculine, but something like a storm in the chicken coop.  I can't go on detailing everything. The best service I can render the reader is to summarise what I perceive as the problems with the whole set (which includes the Overtures).  If you were to audition one symphony at a time and compare with 3-4 others (meaning: you alternate your aural surroundings each day and each audition), you would notice an attitude throughout that is characterised by fast, almost breathess tempi, a penchant for sharp and brusk articulation, a refusal to linger even in slow movements, a striving for exactitude and clarity in orchestral playing, a transparent texturing, and all this under the umbrella of ONE STYLE. You might judge at the end that this benefits Symphonies Nos. 5 and 7; less so Nos. 1, 2, 4 and 8; and is quite detrimental to No. 6 (where leisured tempi are of the esence) and Nos. 3 and 9, which lack any sense of weight and importance. There are moments where this pays off when it succeeds in conveying a sense of exaltation - in the Finales to No. 5 and 9. But overall these moments are exceptional.   The greatest failing of Chailly's set is indicated by my capitalisation of ONE STYLE. Beethoven's symphonies change style unmistakably after No. 2 and after No. 8. This change of perspective on the composer's supreme vehicle of his art is disregarded here. Over the long run, therefore, you begin to discern the fingerprint not of Beethoven, but of Chailly, as if they were his symphonies. In short: I miss completely what is absolutely indispensable to a Beethoven survey: the depiction of the growth of a creative mind. The way Chailly performs No. 1 and 5 and 9, they all SOUND THE SAME, just richer in orchestration and invention as you go on. With this lack of discrimination, you could be forgiven for wondering what is so great about Beethoven's later works.  My advice is: If you are the owner of Masur's set, you would be best advised to keep it. It has been a model for sane and sensitive Beethoven playing for 40 years and will still be around in the next 40 years.  This is not mentioning at least a dozen surveys by even older conductors who are plainly more deeply attuned to Beethoven's mission in his symphonies than Chailly.  On the other hand, there has been a trend of late to de-spiritualise (not to say: debunk) great music. For listeners who feel this way, the Chailly set may well be the answer to their need - for an 'objective' view of the landscape and indeed an exhibition piece of the art of digital recording.
098357670X|5.0|I've done caricatures for years sometimes just winging it. The Parham and Redman books are good for people starting out. This book is like grad school for caricature artists. B004VPAXIY|5.0|As the previous reviewer stated, this is a new release of The Dreaming on Kate's Fish People label, but everything that I've seen indicates that it is not a remaster.  My understanding is that the 2011 releases of The Sensual World and The Red Shoes ARE remastered, but the new releases of The Dreaming and Hounds of Love are NOT.  I've heard that Kate didn't like the sound of Sensual World and Red Shoes resulting from the use of early all-digital recording, and I suspect these got the remaster treatment because of that.  I have the new Sensual World disc, and it does sound much better than the original.  Dreaming and Hounds were well-produced in analog, and I can only assume that she didn't think remastering was going to make that much of a sonic improvement.  I have the mid-90s EMI remaster of Hounds (now out of print) and it doesn't sound that much different IMO.  (It's worth hunting down for the bonus tracks, though.)  So, if you don't have this CD, just get it - it still sounds great.  If you already have an earlier version, however, you won't get an upgrade in sound quality with this.  You will get new packaging and a fresh, new CD though!

The objective then is double: to stop the four horsemen and prevent their misdeeds, and then to capture their four rings.<br /><br />The red Horseman is War and he enjoys getting into a community and making one half believe the other side are demons and vice versa and watch them killing one another to the end. He will lose his fingers and his ring and be defeated. Some well seasoned hunters were taken in by the horseman's antics very easily like Ellen and Jo.<br /><br />The white horseman is Famine and it kills people by making them overeat all the time, even eating one another. He reduces them to their most intimate desire (either their need for sex, food, drinks etc...). Strangely enough he does not live on that satisfaction but he has demons at his service that collect the souls of some dying people and he Famine nourishes himself on these souls carried to him in a special briefcase by a soul collecting demon. Sam and Dean put Famine on a diet by stealing one of these briefcases. But the confrontation is great because, when Famine gives the order to his minions to bring Dean and Sam for him to eat their souls, Sam is able to kill and exorcise all the demons aroundFamine, except Famine of course. Then Famine eats the black smokes of these demons and Sam exorcises these demons' essences out of Famine, killing him at the same time, since Sam's need was demon blood and he can satisfy himself with the essence of these demons (their black smoke). As for Dean, it is said that he never recovered from his time in Hell and has no desire, like a dead man walking.<br /><br />The green horseman is Pestilence. He spreads all kinds of diseases just by touching people, or even wanting them sick. He is living as Doctor Green in an old people's home where he can experiment his talents. He is also working with a certain Brady in Chicago who is preparing the general attack for Death, the fourth and black horseman, by producing and shipping millions of doses of a special vaccine that will spread the Croatoan virus in the population under the disguise of anti-flu vaccination. Pestilence loves the procedure that will make millions sick and death loves it too since it will make them dead eventually.<br /><br />Sam has to manage his anger when he discovers Brady, one of his old high school and college friends, the very one who introduced him to Jessica, is the direct helper of Pestilence. He will eventually kill him. They will be led to where Death is by Crowley, that demon who managed to get the special Colt from Bela, though the Colt has lost all its magic. Dean will have to confront Death and negotiate his ring with him. Death is bored of being Lucifer's slave and he wants to recapture his freedom, so he gives his ring to Dean provided Dean promises he won't prevent Sam from getting Lucifer back into the cage, a double procedure that requires Sam to accept to be Lucifer's vessel, but then to keep enough power to open the cage and jump into it, Dean having to close the cage in his turn afterwards.<br /><br />But all that is not that simple because Michael has chosen Adam as his vessel and the final fight between Lucifer and Michael takes place in an old cemetery in Lawrence, Kansas between Sam and Adam. Dean will disturb the game by bringing the Impala into the picture with old Rock music that makes Sam nostalgic and when Sam/Lucifer is getting ready to kill Dean with a last final blow Sam takes over with his memories and he opens the cage and ... But then Adam/Michael protests because he did not play his part. Never trust an older son (Michael) especially when he is in fact younger and only a half-brother. And there Sam in a last minute of domination before the end takes Adam/Michael down along with himself and Lucifer into the cage.<br /><br />That's the mystery of this series. It refers in a great eruption of details to some well-known stories, like Saint John's Apocalypse, all kinds of apocrypha around this Apocalypse and many other Apocalypses, since apocalypse only means revelation and there are at least three fundamental \\"apocalypses\\" in the Bible, two in the Old Testament and Saint John's. Apocrypha are myriads and no one can know them all, especially since some are still somewhere in some desert in a jar under some stone in a cave. We just don't know which cave. At the same time the brothers state as often as they can that we are free to choose what we want and they pervert the stories they are dealing with. Here it is typical: Michael in Lucifer's cage with Lucifer, older and younger brothers, respectively in the bodies and beings of Adam and Sam, half brothers the first one being the younger one, is a perfect perversion and it will lead to a mountain of complications, in the sixth season of course.<br /><br />But the whole series is so rich that we cannot start telling you every single episode. Let me say that the story is more and more closely knit, very few, if even only one, episodes are off the main line. The trickster was such a side line and yet he is brought back into the center in this season, and what a center indeed. An archangel himself. At the same time there is a slightly irritating over-Christianizing of the story and that is maybe the result of the direct connection with Saint John's Book of Revelation.<br /><br />It is gross and absolutely funny to have a convention of all traditional pagan gods, well not all of them as a matter of fact, but a few nevertheless, and it leads to a pleasurable though perfectly sickening episode. It is surprising that these pagan gods are all killed, except Kali, the only woman in the band. Note the approach is really folkloric because most pagan gods that are still worshipped are not males but are most of them androgynous, like the Hindu gods. Kali is supposed to represent that family but the family has three major gods and all of them are androgynous. This episode is pure horrifying comedy. Especially with the victuals in the freezer being live human beings, one or two being actually served at the dinner table. Appetizing indeed. And yet it is in this episode that the Trickster reveals who he really is and is eliminated from the competition by Lucifer himself.<br /><br />It is also a change of direction for Castiel, who, in this fight between Heaven and Hell, chooses to be on mankind's side, a decision that he will pay by becoming more and more human, losing his grace little by little during the whole season. In an episode, when Dean is transferred by the Angels into the future to see what the Apocalypse will bring to Earth, he is the last remaining Angel on Earth, but a poor one, lost in drugs and women: he had become a real human. However, after being killed by Lucifer in the last episode, he is resurrected by God and given back his grace. During a part of the season, he tries to find God to prevent the Apocalypse from happening and his Father tells him to stop looking for him and that he will not intervene as human matters are no longer his concern.<br /><br />In spite of being closely knit the season is also providing quite a few sidetracks but they are systematically brought back in line in a way or another. The First Annual Supernatural Convention though is both funny and silly. Yet it revives and builds up the Chuck character as the author of the comic books, but we know he is also the prophet of God and writing the next Gospel of the faith. And it is this Chuck who closes the series with his concluding remark: \\"Endings are bad. But nothing really ends, does it?\\" That's what they call suspense I guess. See you next September. Yet this Chuck in-between the various events of the last episode provides us with the history of the famous Impala car that is roaming the roads of TV land searching our mental territories for grotesque demons and all the more grotesque angels.<br /><br />This season is essential though as for the two brothers. Their relation becomes adult enough that they can envisage to be living separate lives and destinies. Dean will survive the events and will have a normal life with Lisa and her/his son Ben. Sam during that time will have a double incestuous relation with his double brother Adam/Michael, being double himself Sam/Lucifer. If Freud is right a relation between two people is in fact a relation between four people, then here a relation between four people is a relation between eight. That means it is very complex, complicated, intricate and fascinating, as for all the combinations you can imagine.<br /><br />Yet all along the two brothers reveal some very interesting things to us.<br /><br />The fact that Dean constantly wants to drop out because he does not have any faith in the whole business, makes him consider he is used more than deciding what he is doing. This develops in him a strange boredom and tiredness that leads to being defeated and walking away. He does it several times and his come-backs become more and more difficult. He is the one who never says a real yes to anyone and the final moment when he says yes to Michael, in his absence what's more, he manages to kill his messenger and escape him, leaving behind a perfectly frightened half portion of what it would take to just imagine being Michael.  And yet, Dean seems to be the only one who makes the choice from his own free will and not to answer a guilty feeling, an angel demand or anything else.<br /><br />Dean suffers from another disease concerning his brother. He treats him like a little brother all the time. True enough Sam treats him like a big brother all the time. They both have to grow up out of boyhood and into adulthood. The first one to do it is Sam, the younger one, because he was baited into demon blood addiction and because he was the one who opened the cage. This tremendous responsibility on the guilty side of his conscience builds in him a new responsibility on the victorious side of his mind: to be the one who can fix his own mistake without the help of big brother even if that means somewhere getting close to killing that big brother and stopping just in time and then disappearing in the limbos of nowhere under an immensity of nowhereness. Though Sam volunteers for that jump into unknown territories, he wants to have the approval of his big brother and his personal and direct decision to get onboard that crazy Luciferian boat leads him to leaving his bigger brother behind, alive luckily.<br /><br />Dean is not that positive. He is more afraid of losing Sam than of losing. He yields to Sam's decision to play the game of Lucifer to trap him, though he may run the risk of killing his own brother in the meantime, a risk that Sam is ready to take because there is a perfectly unfair chance that he may not kill him and stop just before that point. But Dean is ready till and as far as the last minute to die at the hands of his own brother trying to get him back from his Satanic fate. This desire, and I mean desire, to die like that is not a risk he is taking. It is a decision of his because at this moment he wants to die united in his own death to the very fists of his brother killing him. That intensity goes beyond love. Dean at this moment is totally addicted to his relation to his own brother Sam, and only to that relation. He can then step down and move back to Lisa and have a life of oblivion and frustration under the sole light that Sam loved him enough not to kill him. And that peaceful fate for Dean was the strong suggestion Sam made before closing the deal.<br /><br />Dean on the other side is able to widen and open his love to the whole humanity. He is not hunting to kill demons, but to save people. Killing demons is only the easiest method you have to save people. He kills without any pangs of conscience the demons who are endangering humanity, like those at the pharmaceutical plant before the deadly vaccines are shipped out, and yet he will consider his task as done only when he will have saved, one by one, all the humans who got trapped in that factory. The fact that Dean constantly wants to drop out because he does not have any faith in the whole business, makes him consider he is used more than deciding what he is doing. This develops in him a strange boredom and tiredness that leads to being defeated and walking away. He does it several times and his come-backs become more and more difficult. He is the one who never says a real yes to anyone and the final moment when he says yes to Michael, in his absence what's more, he manages to kill his messenger and escape him, leaving behind a perfectly frightened half portion of what it would take to just imagine being Michael.  And yet, Dean seems to be the only one who makes the choice from his own free will and not to answer a guilty feeling, an angel demand or anything else.  Dean suffers from another disease concerning his brother. He treats him like a little brother all the time. True enough Sam treats him like a big brother all the time. They both have to grow up out of boyhood and into adulthood. The first one to do it is Sam, the younger one, because he was baited into demon blood addiction and because he was the one who opened the cage. This tremendous responsibility on the guilty side of his conscience builds in him a new responsibility on the victorious side of his mind: to be the one who can fix his own mistake without the help of big brother even if that means somewhere getting close to killing that big brother and stopping just in time and then disappearing in the limbos of nowhere under an immensity of nowhereness. Though Sam volunteers for that jump into unknown territories, he wants to have the approval of his big brother and his personal and direct decision to get onboard that crazy Luciferian boat leads him to leaving his bigger brother behind, alive luckily.  Dean is not that positive. He is more afraid of losing Sam than of losing. He yields to Sam's decision to play the game of Lucifer to trap him, though he may run the risk of killing his own brother in the meantime, a risk that Sam is ready to take because there is a perfectly unfair chance that he may not kill him and stop just before that point. But Dean is ready till and as far as the last minute to die at the hands of his own brother trying to get him back from his Satanic fate. This desire, and I mean desire, to die like that is not a risk he is taking. It is a decision of his because at this moment he wants to die united in his own death to the very fists of his brother killing him. That intensity goes beyond love. Dean at this moment is totally addicted to his relation to his own brother Sam, and only to that relation. He can then step down and move back to Lisa and have a life of oblivion and frustration under the sole light that Sam loved him enough not to kill him. And that peaceful fate for Dean was the strong suggestion Sam made before closing the deal.  Dean on the other side is able to widen and open his love to the whole humanity. He is not hunting to kill demons, but to save people. Killing demons is only the easiest method you have to save people. He kills without any pangs of conscience the demons who are endangering humanity, like those at the pharmaceutical plant before the deadly vaccines are shipped out, and yet he will consider his task as done only when he will have saved, one by one, all the humans who got trapped in that factory. Altruism is the fodder of a man like Sam, whereas one-pointed purposefulness is the only food Dean can have and he often has some kind of gastric reflux that gives him indigestion and prevents him from eating his mental stomach full. B00005ASHM|5.0|I remember the first time I ever heard 'Pet Sounds.' I was sitting at a beach house in Delaware, just relaxing, and I decided to pop in a copy of 'Pet Sounds' that I just purchased not too long ago (at the time). I shut my eyes, popped the CD in the player, and entered another universe. I was moved to tears (and it takes a LOT for me to cry). This record completely changed my life.   It has taken me forever to write a review on Amazon for 'Pet Sounds,' simply because I couldn't quite find the words to describe Brian Wilson and The Beach Boys' 1966 masterpiece. I still can't honestly. But I'm going to try. But first, in case you don't already understand the background of this record, allow me to \\"clue you in.\\"  In the early 1960s, The Beach Boys consisted of Mike Love, Brian Wilson, Dennis Wilson, Carl Wilson, and David Marks. This lineup released three records from 1962-1963, all consisting of \\"fun in the sun\\"-esque songs, revolving around the surf, sun, cars, and girls. These records symbolized the California surf sound. Marks left the group in 1963, replaced by original member Al Jardine (although both were in the group at the same time, it was soon obvious that Jardine was going to dominate the rhythm guitar spot -- Jardine had been in the group during its earliest incarnation but left in late-1961 to pursue dentistry, only to return less than two years later). With this lineup change, the group slowly moved towards a more complex sound and added tighter vocal harmonies. In 1964, Brian Wilson suffered a nervous breakdown, and stopped touring. The group initially replaced Wilson on the road with future country and television star Glen Campbell on the road, but this was no long term solution. The group released one final album with the five-piece, 'The Beach Boys Today!' before adding Bruce Johnston to the touring lineup as Brian Wilson's replacement, and, as it turned out, in the studio as well.  In 1965, the group released 'Summer Days (And Summer Nights!!),' which is perhaps the precursor to what was to come a year later. This record proved to be their best yet, and showed The Beach Boys maturing as vocalists and lyricists. But even in '65, nobody could have predicted what was to occur a year later.  That's the history lesson.  Brian Wilson was the visionary behind this record. And his vision shines through brilliantly here, to say the least. The vocal harmonies on this record are otherworldly. There are simply no words to describe just how brilliant 'Pet Sounds' is. 'Wouldn't it Be Nice,' 'Sloop John B,' and 'God Only Knows' are the \\"radio hits\\" (notice the quotations -- this really isn't a radio-friendly record by any means) from this album, but these are only part of the mystique of 'Pet Sounds.'   Listen to the haunting harmonies on 'You Still Believe in Me,' and the creative use of sounds by Brian. A honking horn? Brilliant. Mike Love turns out one of his best vocal performances on 'That's Not Me.' To those who say that Mike Love can't sing, listen to 'That's Not Me' again. His vocals here are emotional and almost flawless, and Brian's vocals in the chorus are also just brilliant. I've always loved the guitar sound in the chorus here as well.   The instrumentals are also great. 'Let's Go Away for Awhile' is a dreamy, mysterious piece that features none of The Beach Boys playing on it (this includes Brian Wilson). Wilson arranges it and does it perfectly, but take note that he does not actually participate in the playing. I've always loved the vibraphones on here. This is a neat instrumental. The title track is also quite imaginative, featuring the banging of Coca-Cola cans for percussion. The guitar sound on here is great, as are all of the multiple percussion tracks. This track is extremely complex, and packs a lot into it for a song under 4 minutes.  'Here Today' is another fine performance by Love. This is a love song, and it is probably one of the few tracks here (besides the \\"radio hits\\") that the strictly \\"fun-in-the-sun\\" Beach Boys fans could relate to. While I'll always prefer Love's vocals on 'That's Not Me,' he still sings wonderfully here, and should be praised for his vocal delivery.  And then there are the tearjerkers. 'Don't Talk (Put Your Head On My Shoulder)' is, in my opinion, the most beautiful piece of music ever found on any modern record of any genre. Period. Brian Wilson's vocals here are as close to flawless as one could ever hope to hear. The string arrangements in the middle of the track are perfect, and just suit the track perfectly. Hal Blaine's ever-so-light tapping on cymbal is just perfect for this track. More so than any other track on the record, this song creates a mood. It's slow, romantic, and thought-provoking. This may very well be the most perfect track ever put on tape. And I mean that. 'I Just Wasn't Made for These Times' is also mind-bogglingly beautiful. If you aren't moved by Brian's vocal delivery and the harmonies contained in the middle section, there is something seriously wrong with you (or you are soulless). 'Caroline No' has always been my personal favorite track on the record. While it isn't the most beautiful song on the record, it certainly could be on any other record. Brian's vocals are gut-wrenching and just perfect. You can feel almost every emotion he is trying to convey. You feel the heartbreak in his voice, and it almost stings. But it is also quite beautiful, and the arrangement could not be more perfect.  The hits on this record are also perfect. 'Sloop John B' is an old folk song that Bud & Travis had recorded a few years ago, as had The Kingston Trio. Al Jardine had the idea to put a folk song on the record for a balance. Brian, at first reluctant, agreed, and radically altered the track for the record. This song was a radio hit back in 1966, and while I have read that the track is out of place on the record, I could not disagree more. I think the track is perfect here, as it gives the record a lighter track, despite the song's message being rather sad. (This song would have also fit perfectly on 'Summer Days' in my opinion).  'Wouldn't it Be Nice' has been played many times, and is still a concert staple for all three current touring lineups of The Beach Boys. Tony Asher's lyrics here are great, and Wilson's voice is also in top shape. This song conveys a message of optimism, and is a perfect love song and the ultimate record opener. 'God Only Knows' was one of Carl Wilson's first vocal performances, and it also remains one of his best. Wilson would perform this song in concert with The Beach Boys right up until his death in February 1998. The backing track is perfect, and the harmonies that Brian, Carl, and Bruce Johnston share on the track are just gorgeous.   Lastly, 'I Know There's an Answer' could be interpreted as Brian's take on LSD. It's an interesting track, and it does surprisingly fit on this record. The backing track is extremely complex, featuring a tambourine as its driving rhythm. It's quite interesting. It's not a beauty like some of the other tracks here, but it is certainly a very cool track.  The bonus tracks are also neat. 'Trombone Dixie' appears on some additions. Portions of this track (you can call them \\"recycled bits\\") would later show up on later albums (as backing tracks). 'Hang On to Your Ego' is simply the original version of 'I Know There's an Answer' before Mike Love made Brian Wilson and Tony Asher change the lyrics due to its obvious drug references.  The years following 1966 would see some good times ('Good Vibrations,' released at the end of the year would reach #1, the last Beach Boys track to do so in America until 1988) and bad times (the shelving of the 'SMiLE' album until November 2011, Brian Wilson's breakdown, the disappearance of The Beach Boys on the American charts). But one thing is for sure -- 'Pet Sounds' is brilliant. I think it is the greatest album ever made, and it is probably also my favorite album ever made. It is beautiful, moving, interesting, complex, intricate, gorgeous, and flawless. It is very well engineered and very well played (featuring some of the best session musicians in the business, including drummer Hal Blaine, guitarist Barney Kessel and bassist Carol Kaye). This record is a masterpiece that must be owned by all serious music fans.  I don't care if you like The Beach Boys or not. If you don't own 'Pet Sounds,' you don't have a collection of music that is worthy of mentioning. Plain and simple. Words cannot describe just how brilliant this record really is. Millions have tried to sing its praises (like I am trying to do now), but there really aren't words adequate enough to describe just how wonderful this record is. This is more than a record -- it is almost a religious experience in its own right.  Five stars is not worthy of a record like this. Infinite stars are needed to even *come close* to describing how brilliant this album is. Buy this album IMMEDIATELY if you don't own it already. Buy it and prepare to be blown away by the sheer brilliance that is 'Pet Sounds.'

B005TPCQ5I|4.0|ONE DAY is a gentle surprise of a film.  Though the premise of a 20 year friendship turning into love is not a new one, the finesse the two main actors bring to this story makes it work like a fresh peach. In ONE DAY (we visit St. Swithin's day for 20 years) Dexter (Jim Sturgess) and Emma (Anne Hathaway) formally meet on July 15, 1986 as they graduate from college: each has big dreams for the future, and quite by accident they spend the night together - in a rather platonic way. However, the FastPack allows quick access to the camera in a way similar to a sling bag - slide one strap off, swivel the bag to the front, unzip, and take out / replace the camera.  The top is one large open compartment with a mesh bag to one side. Unzipped, the part the back of the bag - the part that is in contact with your back when you're carrying it - folds down. The bottom compartment is smaller and, in my opinion, not as useful as the FastPack. If you place a DSLR as shown in the photo, there's a fair bit of wasted space around the lens, and forget about having largish lenses in there. I cannot put a Canon 50D with a 24-105mm with its back facing up - it needs to go in horizontally, and after that there's not much space for other lenses.  It would work for a smaller lens, or smaller camera systems. But the difficulty in removing the camera is still a major disadvantage in my books. If it is hard to remove the camera, you probably will not use the camera. If you are not using the camera, why bring it along?  Perhaps there is some use scenario that this would be ideal for, but my personal preference is for the FastPack 200 by the same manufacturer. So, I'd rate it 5 stars for build quality, 3 stars for design and usability as a camera bag.

B004H4XI5O|5.0|I loved this book. It's trying my favorite type - memoir with some good old educational research thrown in for fun.<br /><br />Josh Foer is a reporter. One year he was asked to cover the U.S. Memory Championship, and became facinated with memory training. After hobnobbing with other \\"Memory Athletes\\", he decides to train for the US Championships.<br /><br />This is Josh's story, but it is also the history of memory use. Did you every think about ancient times predating paper and pen and computer and cell phone? People were required to use their memories and trained themselves to make mental lists, memorize literature and keep track of their day to days. There were few external memory techniques, so people needed good internal memory just for pure survival. Foer's charts some of the history - and notes the current techniques vs. ancient ones.<br /><br />In his year of training, Foer also researches and interviews many memory subjects - those who have trained their memory, and those seemingly gifted with an abundant memory. He provides lots of interesting antidotes on these people and meetings and shares how he incorporates certain things into his training techniques.<br /><br />Very good story, and a lot to learn here about what we can do with our minds. Highly recommend, especially if you are a Memoir Geek like me!
1439153663|3.0|Started out good. I was intrigued with the unusual twist of a slave novel: young Irish girl is indentured to a a slave owning family and ends up living with the slaves, thinking their her family.  Admittedly, this tested my ability to suspend disbelief--how could the girl, Lavinia, be quite so clueless? But her life was very disrupted, her whole family ripped from her, that perhaps she was in a kind of fugue. . . Anyway, she develops strong relationships with her slave family, Mama, papa, Belle (the master's daughter and supposed mistress), Frannie and Beattie, the twins who are her age. But she also develops a good relationship with Martha, the mistress of the house who is slowly going mad and often believes Lavinia is her dead sister.   To add a sinister air to the plot, the overseer is a truly horrible person and he and Marshall, the master's son, become fast friends. When the Master finally figures out how bad the overseer is (the master is also clueless and it takes him a really long time), he sends him away, sends Marshall to Williamsburg to get educated, and hires a new overseer. Enter Will, Lavinia's love interest.  The first half of the novel chronicles Lavinia's development until the Master dies and Martha's family comes to take Martha to an insane asylum. They take Lavinia with them because they feel that when Martha gets out she will want Lavinia with her (and, besides, what's this white girl doing with the slaves, they wonder).  Total life turnaround for Lavinia who becomes a Southern young lady, a very obedient Southern Young Lady. All of this I enjoyed. Lavinia lives with her intriguing cousin, a young lady botanist and the novel seems to be heading towards a good love story/coming into herself story.   The novel, for me, falls apart when Lavinia marries Marshall, the son of her original Master. Marshall, the reader knows is a real jerk, abusive and rapist. But Lavinia who continues to be clueless and naive has no idea (gets harder and hard to suspenddisbelief at this point).  Lavinia returns and wants everything to go back to its old ways--and so begins a series of increasingly unrealistic disasters.  What made the last part of the novel irritating and unsatisfying to me was that it made Lavinia seem so wimpy and helpless.  Even her slave family remarks on her inability to act or do anything; and her selfishness leads to some real problems.  And, this helplessness only increases as the novel progresses until at the end, half the characters in the book seem to be screaming, running around and crying.  The novel is told predominantly from Lavinia's perspective, although occasional, short chapters are told from Belle's perspective. Many of the characters are one dimensional and we don't really get to know who they are (mostly the slaves, but Marshall as well). We do get some perspective on their actions from other things that happen to them, but they still didn't feel fully developed to me.

The whole deal unfolds with a depressing air of inevitability: cue zombie birds, post-apocalypse hillbilly-punk survivors (\\"Kin ah kill 'er Maw? I simply can't get away from the idea that Paul W Anderson simply dusted off his Mad Max 2 screenplay and added zombies. I was going to give it a two star rating, but I added a third out of loyalty to the franchise.

1400069181|4.0|I was lucky enough to be chosen as a Books for Bloggers winner recently, and thoroughly enjoyed reading Blake's book. I am not business-minded whatsoever, so the concepts, to me, were new and exciting. I can imagine that someone familiar with business might find some of the book's tips to be old news, but I think that it serves as a great reminder to those in the business world, where values and ethical motivations and greater purposes are often lost. Here is the review I posted on my blog, Inspired:  So I started Blake Mycoskie's book, Start Something That Matters on Monday, and finished it less than 48 hours later. It was so wonderful! It was a quick read, and I highly recommend it to anyone who is looking to start a business, or anyone who currently works with any organization of any kind. In other words, I recommend this book to everyone. Blake starts with the journey that led him to start TOMS - the vacation that inspired him, the drawing in his journal that became a reality, and the people along the way who helped him. As a huge supporter of TOMS (I currently own 10 pairs at last count...and I don't have any plans to stop there), it was amazing to read the story of the company. Blake talks about how important transparency is to his success, and he maintains that throughout the book - every aspect of his history, including mistakes and motivations, are put right out there in the open. I've heard many people criticize TOMS for being a for-profit organization, using their giving as a ploy to attract more business. But Blake makes it clear that TOMS has never claimed to be a non-profit organization, and that's OK! The people working for TOMS all have families to support and need to make money like the rest of us - but in the process, they are improving millions of other lives as well (TOMS recently gave away its 1 millionth pair of shoes!) What if every business model worked this way? (Here's an idea...what if, for every professional sports ticket purchased, the team's owner gave that much money to a charity, instead of fighting with the players about how much they make? But that's a soapbox for another time...)  What I found most refreshing about this book was that it follows the TOMS mission perfectly. TOMS' motto has become \\"One for One,\\" meaning that for every pair of shoes that are purchased, TOMS gives away a pair of shoes to a child in need. (And as of this summer, TOMS now gives eyesight! For every pair of sunglasses purchased, TOMS will either provide prescription glasses, medical treatment, or eye surgery for someone who needs it!) The book is no different: at the most basic level, for every one of Blake's books purchased, Random House Publishers donates a children's book to a child in need. But even more than that, the essence of the book is to pass on knowledge and experience to other people who want to \\"start something that matters.\\" In the last chapter of the book, Blake talks about his ultimate goal at TOMS. Originally, he says, it was to create a for-profit company that also improves the lives of children and relieves their suffering. But now, he says, his goal is \\"to influence other people to go out into the world and have a positive impact, to inspire others to start something that matters...\\" (amazing how that worked out, huh? It's like he knew I was going to post this on my blog!) At its core, this book is for the benefit of others. As Blake says, \\"we are all born with the ability to improve another person's life,\\" and he is doing everything he can to do just that, and he challenges us to do the same. He closes by saying that \\"the ultimate success of this book will be measured...by the number of people it inspires\\" (I swear, he wrote this book for me!)

B005ZHB84U|5.0|The first \\"Sherlock Holmes\\" score caught me completely off guard with how different and completely opposite of Zimmer it was and ended being my favorite score that year. For the sequel, Zimmer goes even further by delivering one of the most unique and addicting scores I've heard since his score for the first movie.<br /><br />The album starts off with the Sherlock Holmes theme in almost the same manner as the last. This time with some pauses here and there. A somber opening that brings back that great theme. It's like the calm before the very crazy storm that is the rest of the tracks on this incredible album.<br /><br />The next track introduces a new theme which I'm guessing is a theme for the bad guy in the movie? Starts off with low instruments before being picked up by what I could have sworn was a bass clarinet. A bass clarinet in a Zimmer score? Sheer bloody panic. Other than the clarinet, another instrument that I never thought I'd hear in modern day Zimmer score was the bassoon, which is featured very well on \\"The Mycroft Suite\\".<br /><br />\\"Tick Tock\\" is easily one of my favorite tracks on the album. It takes a while for it get going, but once it kicks, it really starts getting fun. I'm sure the ticking sound will make sense once I see the movie, but for now it's a very cool sound that's almost present throughout the track as it gets crazier and crazier. One of the few action tracks I believe, but very well done.<br /><br />I remember on the last \\"Holmes\\" score there was a track called \\"I never woke up in handcuffs before\\" which I thought was as crazy as Zimmer could ever be in terms of playfulness. Well this score proved me wrong. There are tracks on here that I had to stop listening to about halfway through to make sure I was listening to the right soundtrack.  \\"Chess\\" is an early and great example of this.<br /><br />The track \\"Chess\\" is not only my personal track on the album, but just might be my favorite track of the year. Listened to it so many times and it still makes me smile. I love how weird it starts, and that odd brass and snares at the 17 sec. mark before going in a very unique and crazy direction. I LOVE this track. Just incredible anyway you cut it. Would pay good money to see the look on the faces of the orchestra when they saw the sheet music for the first time.<br /><br />But as crazy as \\"Chess \\"is, I think \\"It's So Overt it's Covert\\" might top it. I can just picture Zimmer telling the orchestra to get supremely wasted then try to play the music, which is kind of what it sounds like. The clarinet just flies around like a bee trying to find a flower whiles the rest of the instruments try their best to play the Sherlock Holmes theme. I love this track; it's just so weird and out there and very encouraging to me that Zimmer was allowed to get away with this. Very refreshing to see this kind of experimenting in a big budget movie.<br /><br />\\"Romanian Wind\\" is another one that's far out there. Like the Cantina band in \\"Star Wars\\" but on acid or some kind of illegal narcotics. Don't know the best way to describe it, but it's a very interesting piece of music that I'm still not sure if it was written or if the ensemble just improved it but I love it. \\"He's all Me, Me, Me\\" is an odd one too. I really hope they have a lengthy behind the scenes on the blu ray for the music, die of happiness to see the orchestra performing this music.<br /><br />\\"Did You Kill My Wife\\" sounds like the type of music you would hear in a very dramatic Western as the hero is making a speech or walking into town. It's essentially the brass being led by a beautiful trumpet. The source track \\"Two Mules for Sister Sara\\" is a bit distracting at first, but guess it will make sense once I see the movie<br /><br />Fans of Zimmer's action music will appreciate \\"To the Opera\\", which is an action track but with a very unique twist. It's an opera action track if that makes any sense. Throughout out the pounding of the orchestra you can hearan opera singer singing Mozart whiles the orchestra keeps up. It sounds like it won't work, but Zimmer makes it work so well on here. Can't wait to see the movie to see what scene that goes with. \\"Red Book\\" would be another great action track that I can't wait to see the movie to see what it goes with. Starts slow, but after about a minute the action kicks in. And dare say it has an Elfman vibe to it.<br /><br />The closest thing to subtle on the album would be the very sweet \\"Memories of Sherlock\\". Guessing it's for either something romantic in the movie or a slow motion explosion or something. It is very nice to hear though, a nice break from the off the wall stuff. A short break but still.<br /><br />I'm guessing \\"The End?\\" is for the end of the movie. *shocking I know*. It ends just like the first movie did with an energetic and bombastic version of the Holmes theme. A very nice ending to a really great album. Now I should probably say something about the remix track but um...yeah<br /><br />I really love this score. It's unique, it's experimental, it's fun and it has some great action. Don't know if it's the best of the year since I haven't heard \\"War Horse\\" yet, but so far it's my favorite. The premise was great.  A man traveling through time to prevent the Kennedy assassination.  Interwoven were the stories of how the traveler would right a few other wrongs along the way.  For those of you who seem drawn to this novel because it brings back memories of the 50's and 60's, it does that, but in a rather dark way. I found myself skimming over much of that material in an effort to get to the meat of the story.  I think a good editor would have recognized that factor and would have done a bit of editing, but, maybe at this level of publishing, you just don't tell Stephen King how to write.  Face it, anything he publishes is going to hit the best seller list.  In an interview, even Stephen King said he will never tackle this type of subject matter again.
